# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

## Your Task

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the compliance domain.

## Available Resources

- `sg-evals/kafka--0753c489` (apache/kafka)

- `sg-evals/camel--1006f047` (apache/camel)

65-assistanttext-
Perfect! I've completed a comprehensive audit of the Kafka log retention policy enforcement. Here's a summary of what I found:

## Key Findings

I identified **10 core Java source files** and **28 key symbols** that enforce log retention policy in Apache Kafka. The system works through a coordinated multi-layer pipeline:

### Core Components:

1. **UnifiedLog.java** - The primary enforcement engine with three deletion strategies:
   - `deleteRetentionMsBreachedSegments()` - Enforces time-based retention
   - `deleteRetentionSizeBreachedSegments()` - Enforces size-based retention  
   - `deleteLogStartOffsetBreachedSegments()` - Handles offset-based cleanup

2. **LogConfig.java** - Configuration management with fields like `retentionSize`, `retentionMs`, and remote variants

3. **ServerLogConfigs.java** - Defines configuration constants:
   - `LOG_RETENTION_TIME_MILLIS_CONFIG` - Time-based retention setting
   - `LOG_RETENTION_BYTES_CONFIG` - Size-based retention setting
   - `LOG_CLEANUP_INTERVAL_MS_CONFIG` - Check interval (default 5 minutes)

4. **LogManager.scala** - Orchestrates periodic cleanup tasks

5. **DynamicBrokerConfig.scala** - Validates local vs. remote retention interactions

6. **RemoteLogManager.java** - Extends retention enforcement to remote-stored segments with `RemoteLogRetentionHandler`

The answer has been saved to `/workspace/answer.json` with complete details about files, symbols, execution chains, and a comprehensive narrative explaining how the retention system works.

            "content": "594:     <p>\n595:     This retention policy can be set per-topic, so a single cluster can have some topics where retention is enforced by size or time and other topics where retention is enforced by compaction.\n596:     <p>\n597:     This functionality is inspired by one of LinkedIn's oldest and most successful pieces of infrastructure&mdash;a database changelog caching service called <a href=\"https://github.com/linkedin/databus\">Databus</a>.\n598:     Unlike most log-structured storage systems Kafka is built for subscription and organizes data for fast linear reads and writes.             "content": "591:     <p>\n592:     Log compaction is a mechanism to give finer-grained per-record retention, rather than the coarser-grained time-based retention. The idea is to selectively remove records where we have a more recent update with the\n593:     same primary key. This way the log is guaranteed to have at least the last state for each key.\n594: ",
            "endLine": 594,
            "startLine": 591
          },
          {
            "content": "553:     <p>\n554:     So far we have described only the simpler approach to data retention where old log data is discarded after a fixed period of time or when the log reaches some predetermined size. This works well for temporal event\n555:     data such as logging where each record stands alone. However an important class of data streams are the log of changes to keyed, mutable data (for example, the changes to a database table).\n556:     <p>\n557:     Let's discuss a concrete example of such a stream.
